13)
any other factors or information.
NOTE 1
Estimated cost of each option prepared using whole life costing techniques (see Section 4 of this
document and CD 355 [Ref 3.N]).
NOTE 2
Other factors or information can include a corrosion report (if available).
7.21.2
Where it is proposed to monitor concrete deterioration rather than promoting a repair scheme, a
predicted rate of deterioration, risk of structural instability, and recommended intervention date should
be reported in the SMOR.
7.22
The SMOR shall be submitted to the TAA for agreement.
7.22.1
The format of the SMOR may be based the model form of Structures Options Report in CG 300 [Ref
56.N].
Preliminary design of repairs
7.23
The minimum extent of concrete repairs shall comprise the areas where cover concrete is delaminated
and areas where concrete has spalled.
NOTE
Repair of deteriorated concrete and reinforcement can prove difficult where access is restricted.
Guidance is given in CS 466 [Ref 37.I] for concrete half-joints and CS 467 [Ref 36.I] for deck hinges.
7.23.1
Areas of intact but chloride-contaminated concrete where the risk of reinforcement corrosion is high
should be treated by either of the following methods:
1)
removal of the intact but chloride-contaminated concrete which is assessed as having a high risk
of corrosion;
2)
protect reinforcement within intact but chloride-contaminated by using galvanic anodes fixed to
reinforcement within the repair area.
7.23.2
Areas of intact but carbonated concrete, where the risk of reinforcement corrosion due to carbonation is
high, should be treated with an anti-carbonation coating in accordance with CD 369 [Ref 55.N].
NOTE
Galvanic anodes attached to reinforcement within a repaired area can protect reinforcement within
adjacent concrete up to 0.3m away from the edge of the repair.
7.23.3
As an alternative, impressed current cathodic protection (ICCP) may be used to protect reinforcement
within intact concrete contaminated by chlorides.
NOTE
ICCP can be appropriate where chloride contamination of intact concrete is more extensive, see CD
370 [Ref 7.I], but the cost of inspection, monitoring and maintenance can be significant.
7.23.4
Defects in a structure assessed with a high reserve of strength may be repaired to allay third party
concerns about spalling or delaminated concrete potentially causing harm to the public.
NOTE
Visible concrete defects sometimes look worse than they are, but if left unrepaired can result in
reputational harm to the Overseeing Organisation and additional work to respond to reports from the
public.
7.23.5
Temporary holding repairs may be undertaken to reduce deterioration, pending design and construction
of a full repair scheme at a later date.
NOTE
Durability of holding repairs can be significantly less than a permanent repair.
Galvanic anodes within repairs to control incipient anode effect
7.24
The risk of corrosion to reinforcement within intact but chloride-contaminated concrete adjacent to
delaminated areas shall be assessed and conclusions recorded.
NOTE
Accelerated reinforcement corrosion within chloride contaminated concrete adjacent to a new repair is
known as the incipient anode effect.
7.25
Options for mitigating a high risk of corrosion shall be appraised and conclusions recorded.
55
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 57 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
7.25.1
The extent and concentration of chloride contamination within intact concrete adjacent to delaminated
areas should be measured before proposing galvanic anodes within repairs.
NOTE
Galvanic anodes placed within repair patches can control the incipient anode effect.
7.26
Galvanic anodes shall not be used in the following situations:
1)
where the chloride contamination of intact concrete surrounding proposed repairs is less than
0.4% by mass of cement;
2)
where an impressed current cathodic protection system overlaying or underlaying the repair is
proposed;
3)
within repair patches associated with mechanical, impact or fire damage, unless the adjacent
intact concrete is chloride contaminated.
7.27
Design of galvanic anodes within a concrete repair shall be undertaken by an Engineer with the
qualifications and experience required by BS EN ISO 15257 [Ref 8.N] .
NOTE
An array of galvanic anodes provided inside the perimeter of repairs is unlikely to meet the
requirements of cathodic protection as defined by BS EN ISO 12696 [Ref 9.I], but they can prevent
initiation of corrosion to reinforcement immediately adjacent to the repair patches.
7.27.1
Galvanic anodes fixed to reinforcement should be designed to prevent initiation of corrosion on
reinforcement located within intact but contaminated concrete up to 0.30 m away from the edge of the
repair patch for at least 10 years.
NOTE 1
Galvanic anodes are electrically connected to existing reinforcement within the repair patch to control
the initiation of corrosion in reinforcement located in adjacent chloride-contaminated but intact concrete.
NOTE 2
The life of galvanic anodes installed within repairs depends on the resistivity of the parent concrete and
new repair concrete. In lower resistivity concrete, anodes provide more current and better protection,
but the anode life is reduced.
7.27.2
A longer design life up to 15 years may be specified with the agreement of the TAA.
7.27.3
The performance of an array of galvanic anodes fixed to reinforcement within a repair patch should not
require monitoring by permanent reference electrode.
NOTE 1
The current supplied by the anodes varies according to demand from the surrounding concrete, but
cannot be increased if later measured as insufficient. Lack of concrete delamination for 10 years or
longer is definitive confirmation of adequate performance.
NOTE 2
Potential for adequate performance of anodes can be inferred by carrying out a corrosion potential
(half-cell) survey outside the repair patches before removing defective concrete, and again following
repairs including galvanic anodes. Evidence of initial protection is a positive shift in electrical potential.
Other cathodic protection systems
7.28
Other cathodic protection systems proposed in conjunction with concrete repairs shall be designed in
accordance with CD 370 [Ref 7.I].
7.28.1
Where a cathodic protection system overlaying repairs is proposed, existing concrete containing
conductive fibres and existing resin-based concrete with a resistivity greater than 150 kΩ.cm should be
replaced as recommended in CPA TN19 [Ref 45.I].
Substandard concrete cover
7.29
Where the existing concrete cover to reinforcement is substandard, a corrosion risk assessment shall
be undertaken to decide whether remedial action to restore cover is appropriate.
NOTE
Design standards for concrete cover to reinforcement have been updated many times with a gradual
increase in recommended cover, see CS TR70 [Ref 26.I] for more information.
7.29.1
Increasing concrete cover for additional corrosion protection to reinforcement may be required where
existing concrete cover is less than 25mm thick.

CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
NOTE
Further information for methods preserving or restoring passivity of reinforcement can be found in table
1 of BS EN 1504-9 [Ref 38.N].
7.29.2
Where reinforcement cover is substandard on the top surface of a concrete deck, the presence of deck
waterproofing may be used as evidence of additional corrosion protection in the corrosion risk
assessment.
7.29.3
Depth of existing cover to reinforcement on a concrete deck should be at least 20 mm for waterproofing
of a concrete deck to be used as evidence of additional corrosion protection.
NOTE
Where a cathodic protection system consisting of a surface-mounted anode within a cementitious
overlay is provided, sub-standard concrete cover would not need to be increased unless it is less than
15mm.
7.30
Where repair to restore or increase cover to reinforcement is proposed, cover shall be restored with a
cementitious repair material to the minimum requirements recommended by BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N] with
a ∆c of zero in accordance with BS 8500-1 [Ref 12.N].
NOTE 1
Cementitious repair materials are type CC and type PCC in accordance with BS EN 1504-1 [Ref 37.N].
NOTE 2
Type PC reactive polymer repair products in accordance with BS EN 1504-1 [Ref 37.N] are resin-based
and do not provide the required corrosion protection to the reinforcement.
7.30.1
The proposed repairs for the restoration of concrete should provide a design working life equivalent to
or greater than the remaining service life of the structure.
7.30.2
An increase of concrete cover to reinforcement on a deck top surface should be provided across the
entire deck surface.
NOTE
Localised concrete protrusions on a deck surface provided to increase cover can be vulnerable to
damage during later work to replace deck waterproofing.
7.31
The design of repairs to restore or increase cover to reinforcement shall include details for the repair
material to be keyed into the existing concrete in order to achieve the required adhesion between the
repair material and the concrete substrate.
7.31.1
The design of repairs to restore or increase cover to reinforcement may include removal of existing
concrete substrate to expose the reinforcement in order to enable the repair material to encompass the
reinforcement.
Concrete injection
7.32
The cause of significant cracking in concrete shall be investigated before planning repair options.
NOTE 1
Cracking can indicate structural integrity is adversely affected and/or durability has been reduced.
NOTE 2
Construction of some structures can predate the publication of modern codes for controlling durability
by limiting maximum crack width.
NOTE 3
Provision of nominal secondary reinforcement was sometimes insufficient to prevent early thermal
cracking.
NOTE 4
More information about causes of cracking can be found in CS TR44 [Ref 49.I].
7.32.1
The investigation should comprise a full survey of cracks (length, width and depth).
NOTE
Light grit blasting of the concrete surface prior to inspecting cracks can make them more visible and
reveal other smaller defects.
7.33
Cracking caused by concrete delamination following reinforcement corrosion shall be rectified using a
conventional concrete repair.
7.33.1
Delaminated concrete has already partially debonded from the base concrete, and concrete injection
techniques should not be used for repair.
57
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 59 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
7.34
Treatment of concrete cracking using concrete injection techniques shall follow the recommendations in
Annex A of BS EN 1504-10 [Ref 41.N] and in 'Repair of concrete structures with reference to BS EN
1504' CS TR69 [Ref 48.N].
7.34.1
Concrete injection techniques may be used for treatment of cracks in existing concrete when the cracks
are wider than 0.3mm.
NOTE
Fine cracks, typically less than 0.3 mm, which are not considered to contribute to the durability of
concrete and corrosion of reinforcement in accordance with CS TR44 [Ref 49.I], can be monitored and
sufficient time allowed for self-healing prior to concrete injection being carried out.
7.34.2
Repair of cracked concrete may be achieved by structurally stitching the sides of crack together using a
force transmitting material, type F complying with BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N]or creating a formal
movement joint and filling it with ductile material, type D.
NOTE
There is a risk of causing or extending cracking because of the applied fluid pressures during injection.
Vacuum-assisted injection techniques can lessen the risk.
7.34.3
Where cracks are widely spaced and approximately parallel, new formal movement joints may be
created and filled with ductile material, type D complying with BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N].
7.35
Proposals for sealing cracks which leak water using swelling fitted filling products Type S complying
with BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N], and other techniques not covered by BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref
39.N]shall be subject to the technical approval of the TAA.
Void grouting in tendon ducts
7.36
Void grouting of tendon ducts shall not be undertaken as a repair or strengthening option, unless
otherwise agreed with the Overseeing Organisation.
NOTE 1
Void grouting is the injection of grout into voids left in tendon ducts after the original grout has
hardened, and not to be confused with regrouting which means the reinjection of grout into ducts while
the original grout is still fluid.
NOTE 2
More advice about void grouting in tendon ducts can be found in CS TR72 [Ref 20.I].
Surface protection systems
7.37
The use of surface protection systems in conjunction with concrete repairs shall be in accordance with
CD 369 [Ref 55.N].
7.37.1
Where testing indicates concrete carbonation has penetrated to within 10mm of the reinforcement, and
some spalling has occurred, anti-carbonation coatings may applied on top of the repaired concrete with
the agreement of the TAA.
7.38
Other surface protection systems in accordance with Principles 1 or 2 of Table 1 in BS EN 1504-9 [Ref
38.N] used in conjunction with concrete repairs shall not be permitted.
Technical approval
7.39
Technical approval of the repair proposals shall be in accordance with CG 300 [Ref 56.N].
7.40
Where relaxations to the CG 300 [Ref 56.N] category are proposed, reasons shall be presented to and
agreed with the TAA before detailed design commences.
Maintaining structural safety during repair work
7.41
The capacity of structural elements, and global stability of the structure for the temporary conditions
shall be assessed in accordance with CS 454 [Ref 5.N]during the design process.
7.41.1
The rate and sequence of concrete removal should be planned to ensure structural integrity and
continuity of load transference are maintained in the structure and structural elements.
58
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 60 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
NOTE 1
The removal of defective concrete from a concrete substructure element which is usually subjected to
compressive loading can affect the continuity of load transference and compromise structural integrity.
NOTE 2
Concrete removal in a tension zone can overstress the existing exposed reinforcement because
anchorage length is reduced, and bar section can also be reduced by corrosion.
7.41.2
Where deterioration is severe and extensive repairs are necessary, phasing of the repair work,
temporary propping of structural elements or traffic restrictions may be necessary while repairs are
carried out.
NOTE
The risk to structural stability can be more serious where compressive stress applied to a structural
member in service is close to the allowable limit. e.g. small diameter slender columns.
7.42
Risks associated with concrete removal and construction of repairs shall be identified, reviewed and
mitigated during the design process.
NOTE 1
Risks can include the following:
1)
damage to vulnerable structural elements, fixings or attachments;
2)
structural collapse,
3)
falling debris,
4)
construction activities associated with maintenance and repair; or
5)
implementation of traffic management while carrying out repair works.
NOTE 2
Those affected can include users of the road network, and those undertaking the work, including
supervisors, inspectors and visitors.
NOTE 3
Mitigation measures for risks associated with concrete removal and construction of repairs include the
implementation of temporary protection measures such as propping or protective enclosures.
7.43
Sequencing restrictions and hold points for phased concrete removal shall be provided as design
outputs in accordance with Section 10 of this document to mitigate the risk of overbreaking the existing
concrete and adversely affecting the structural integrity of the structure.
7.44
Where removal of defective concrete is planned in structural elements identified as high risk e.g.
pre-stressed or post-tensioned elements or half-joints, the residual structural capacity shall be
assessed for each construction stage during the repair works.
NOTE 1
The strength of pre-stressed or post-tensioned concrete elements can be significantly weakened by
removal of defective concrete as the majority of concrete is in compression.
NOTE 2
The strength of half-joints can be adversely affected by either deterioration or the method of repair
undertaken, e.g. repairs close to strand anchorages can initiate loss of pre-stress, or significant loss of
half-joint strength through debonding or cropping of critical bars within half-joints such as longitudinal
soffit bars and diagonal bars.
7.44.1
The risk of damage to strands and reduced continuity of load transfer though concrete associated with
methods of removing defective concrete should be appraised and mitigated to ensure structural
integrity is maintained.
NOTE 1
Concrete can be removed by high pressure water jetting to avoid mechanical damage. Where there is
a risk of demolition water entering voided tendon duct, demolition can be done using light mechanical,
electrical or hand-held tools.
NOTE 2
Removal of concrete using mechanical breakers can cause vibration in the remaining concrete and
result in micro-cracking. Reinforcement bond can be reduced by micro cracking.
Materials, products and systems for repairing concrete
Concrete repair materials and products
7.45
The design of repairs to deteriorated concrete highway structures shall only incorporate concrete repair
products that are suitable for their intended use in accordance with BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N], BS EN
1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N], BS EN 1504-6 [Ref 36.N]or BS EN 1504-7 [Ref 40.N].
59
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 61 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
NOTE 1
Proprietary products are manufactured for concrete repair applications in accordance with BS EN
1504-3 [Ref 42.N], BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N], BS EN 1504-6 [Ref 36.N]or BS EN 1504-7 [Ref
40.N]as relevant and are subject to the mandatory requirements for designated standards given in GC
101 [Ref 22.I].
NOTE 2
A proprietary cement-based repair concrete or mortar is often used for restoring small to medium sized
areas to a depth of up to 150 mm.
7.46
The design of concrete repairs shall not include the use of bonding primers on prepared concrete
substrates unless otherwise recommended by the concrete repair product manufacturer's instructions.
7.47
Strength class R1 concrete repair products in accordance with BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N]shall not be
used for the design of concrete repairs to concrete highway structures.
NOTE
Strength classes R1 to R4 for concrete repair products are explained in BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N].
7.48
Strength class R2 concrete repair products in accordance with BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N]shall not be
used for the design of concrete repairs that are structural in nature.
7.48.1
Strength class R2 concrete repair products may be used for the design of non-structural concrete
repairs provided the short-term elastic modulus of a product is similar to the existing concrete.
7.49
Concrete repairs that are structural in nature shall be designed using strength class R4 or R3 concrete
repair products in accordance with BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N]to provide similar compressive strength to
the existing concrete.
NOTE
The highest strength category in BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N] is R4 which requires a 28-day characteristic
strength of at least 45 MPa .
7.49.1
Where large volume replacement of concrete is proposed for the repairs to a deteriorated concrete
highway structure, the design of the concrete repairs may use designed structural concrete that is
compatible with the existing concrete, in accordance with CD 350 [Ref 72.N] instead of proprietary
concrete repair products.
NOTE 1
Large volume repairs can include replacement of concrete in parapet supporting elements or a full
depth of concrete deck embedding a large transverse mechanical movement joint.
NOTE 2
For large volume replacement of concrete using designed structural concrete in accordance with CD
350 [Ref 72.N], BS 8500-2 [Ref 13.N]allows the use of small size aggregate, which is suitable for
narrow spaces to be filled with repair concrete.
7.49.2
For large volume replacement of concrete using designed structural concrete, the designer should
check the existing reinforcement can resist shrinkage stresses to avoid cracking during the early phase
of concrete maturing and strength gain.
NOTE
The check of the existing reinforcement is needed as there is the risk that the designed structural
concrete is unable to resist shrinkage stresses, unlike typical proprietary concrete repair mixes which
contain additives to limit shrinkage.
Reinforcing bars and dowels in concrete repairs
7.50
New or replacement carbon-steel reinforcing bars with a similar or higher grade of steel to existing
reinforcing bars and designed in accordance with CD 350 [Ref 72.N]shall be used within concrete
repairs.
NOTE
Existing bars are generally made of mild or high-tensile carbon steel.
7.50.1
New or replacement stainless-steel dowels designed in accordance with CD 372 [Ref 17.N]may be
used within concrete repairs, where the risk of bi-metallic corrosion between existing and new bars is
low or with mitigation measures to reduce the risk of bi-metallic corrosion.
NOTE 1
The difference in electrical potential between carbon steel and stainless steel can result in accelerated
corrosion of carbon steel where the existing concrete contains high levels of chloride or low alkalinity.
60
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 62 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
NOTE 2
Proprietary construction products are generally used for anchoring reinforcing bars or dowels and for
coating exposed reinforcement.
7.51
Reinforcing bar connections by steel mechanical couplers shall be designed in accordance with CD
350 [Ref 72.N]using steel mechanical couplers rated fatigue Class D in accordance with BS 8597 [Ref
50.N]and with a diameter and similar material to suit to the existing reinforcement.
NOTE
Certificates confirming coupler fatigue class rating can be found on the websites: CARES (website)
[Ref 10.I] or BBA (website) [Ref 13.I].
7.52
Grouted couplers shall not be used in the design of reinforcing bar connections in concrete repairs.
Materials and products for concrete repairs incorporating galvanic anode and other cathodic
protection systems
7.53
The design of galvanic anode systems to control incipient anode effect shall be undertaken using zinc
core anode units that are either:
1)
tied directly to existing reinforcement and embedded within the proposed concrete repair, referred
to as Type 1A; or
2)
installed in backfilled holes drilled into the existing concrete substrate within the proposed
concrete repair and electrically connected to existing reinforcement, referred to as Type 1B.
7.54
Concrete repair materials proposed in the design of concrete repairs incorporating galvanic anodes and
other cathodic protection systems shall be compatible with the proposed galvanic anodes and other
cathodic protection systems.
7.55
The electrical resistivity of concrete repair materials proposed in the design concrete repairs
incorporating type 1A galvanic anodes shall not be less than 5kΩ.cm and not greater than 15kΩ.cm .
7.56
The electrical resistivity of concrete repair materials proposed in the design concrete repairs
incorporating type 1B galvanic anodes or ICCP systems shall not exceed the greater of
1)
100kΩ.cm ; or
2)
the electrical resistivity limit set out in the type 1B galvanic anodes or ICCP systems
manufacturer's instructions.
7.57
The following concrete repair materials shall not be used in the design of concrete repairs which
incorporate galvanic anodes and other cathodic protection systems:
1)
resin-based repair mortars;
2)
repair products containing conductive additives, admixtures and fibres.
7.57.1
The electrical resistivity of concrete repair materials proposed in the design concrete repairs
incorporating type 1B galvanic anodes or ICCP systems should be similar to the electrical resistivity of
the concrete substrate as recommended in BS EN ISO 12696 [Ref 9.I].
Detailed design of repairs
7.58
Concrete repairs shall be detailed to ensure the repair is and continues to be an integrated part of the
repaired structure.
NOTE 1
Placement of repair concrete around reinforcement restores an alkaline environment, and cleaning the
existing reinforcement (e.g. wet or dry grit blasting) removes contaminants so there is no longer a risk
of reinforcement corrosion.
NOTE 2
Structural repairs are secured in place mechanically by encompassing the reinforcement, and by
adhesion of the repair material onto a roughened and pre-soaked substrate.
7.58.1
For the top surface of concrete decks covered by road surfacing, indicative extents of anticipated
repairs should be provided on design drawings.
61
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 63 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
NOTE 1
Anticipated repairs for the top surface of concrete decks covered by road surfacing can be identified by
defects to the existing road surfacing, waterproofing or exposed concrete, including the following:
1)
cracking;
2)
damage from road planers;
3)
boot or vehicle wheel marks;
4)
hollow sounding area; or
5)
concaved areas of road surfacing.
NOTE 2
While providing indicative extents of anticipated repairs to the top surface of concrete decks covered by
road surfacing is a speculative exercise, it can help to avoid delays in construction during lane closures
of short duration.
7.59
Repairs shall be structural when located above public highways and other areas used by the public.
NOTE
A well executed structural repair can minimise the risk of premature failure due to debonding.
7.60
The required depth of breakout behind reinforcing bars shall be clearly indicated in the design outputs.
NOTE
Specifying a dimension of breakout behind bars allows the contractor to choose a repair product with
an appropriate aggregate size.
7.60.1
Concrete removal should be detailed to extend at least 25 mm behind the outermost reinforcement, so
the repair achieves a good mechanical key.
7.60.2
The depth of removal behind the outer main reinforcement may be reduced to an absolute minimum of
15mm where there is more than one level of reinforcement, provided the removal depth is not less than
2.5 times the maximum aggregate size of replacement concrete.
NOTE
Information and guidance about techniques of removing defective concrete (e.g. high pressure water
jetting, mechanical breakout) can be found on the website: StCA (Website) [Ref 44.I].
7.60.3
Where it is impracticable for a repair to encompass the reinforcement, partially-structural repairs may
be used, with agreement from the TAA, by drilling and resin fixing small diameter stainless steel dowels
into the existing concrete.
NOTE 1
The method can be suitable for minimising removal of concrete for repair of pre-stressed or
post-tensioned concrete beams.
NOTE 2
Durability of superficial structural repairs can be affected by freeze thaw cycles unless additional
reinforcement bond is provided e.g. stainless steel dowels with mesh reinforcement.
NOTE 3
A series of unconnected steel dowels can be incompatible with a CP system if used.
7.60.4
Small diameter stainless-steel dowels should be detailed to reduce the risk of damage during
installation to existing tensioned strands.
7.61
Concrete repairs shall be detailed to promote durability.
7.61.1
Elastic strain characteristics of new repairs should be compatible with the existing concrete.
NOTE 1
It is important the elastic modulus of a repair is similar to the estimated short-term elastic modulus of
the parent concrete to minimise shear stresses at the interface and improve repair durability.
NOTE 2
Compatibility of elastic modulus can be particularly relevant for structural members predominantly
subjected to compressive forces in service e.g. columns, and locations subject to frequent cycles of
traffic loading e.g. decks.
7.61.2
Where the upper surface of a concrete element is being repaired, the final surface should be sloped to
shed water if feasible.
7.61.3
Where repair areas are large, the risk of early thermal cracking should be assessed and mitigated in
accordance with CIRIA C766 [Ref 14.N].
62
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 64 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
7. Protect and repair strategy
7.61.4
Where structural analysis shows that existing reinforcement in a concrete structure does not provide
required restraint against shrinkage and early thermal cracking, additional reinforcement should be
provided in the design of concrete repairs to control cracking of the repair concrete.
NOTE
The cement content of current cementitious repair products can be higher than that used in a 1960s
concrete, resulting in shrinkage forces and early thermal cracking effects that exceed the capacity of
the existing reinforcement.
7.61.5
Staged construction of large area repairs may be required to avoid shrinkage or thermal cracking.
7.61.6
Designed structural concrete in accordance with CD 350 [Ref 72.N] may be used instead of proprietary
repair concrete products in the following situations:
1)
replacing the concrete for the entire element where the depth of concrete replacement extends
well beyond the rear of the outer mat of reinforcement; or
2)
where a large volume of concrete is required to repair the element.
NOTE
Replacement of concrete for entire cantilevers or parapet stringcourses with more than a total depth of
150mm for each face requiring recasting is an example of situations where designed structural
concrete is used instead of proprietary repair concrete products.
7.61.7
Where a designed mix flowable concrete in accordance with CD 350 [Ref 72.N] is proposed for large
areas or anticipated repairs of decks, the mix should be designed to achieve similar performance
characteristics to BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N] products.
7.62
The design outputs for a designed mix flowable concrete for large areas or anticipated repairs of decks
shall be provided in accordance with CD 350 [Ref 72.N].
7.63
The design outputs for a designed mix flowable concrete for large areas or anticipated repairs of decks
shall include testing requirements to demonstrate the required performance characteristics in
accordance with BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N] can be achieved before and following construction.
7.64
Concrete repair patches shall be detailed to achieve a neat appearance.
7.64.1
The perimeter of repair patches should be shaped as a square, rectangular or a rectilinear polygon.
7.65
Where the section loss of the reinforcement is greater than the acceptable limit, bars shall be replaced
or supplemented.
NOTE
The default acceptable section loss limit is 10%, an alternative acceptable limit of section loss can be
determined through assessment.
7.65.1
Additional or supplementary new reinforcing bars may be spliced onto existing corroded bars by
lapping or mechanical coupling to restore section strength.
7.66
The splicing of new reinforcing bars onto existing reinforcement shall not be designed as welded
connections unless agreed otherwise with the TAA.
7.67
Where welding of replacement bars is proposed within the repaired concrete section, a fatigue
verification of the welds shall be carried out in accordance with BS EN 1992-1-1 [Ref 20.N].
7.67.1
Fatigue verification of the reinforced concrete section at a reinforcement welded joint should
demonstrate that the applied fatigue loading is less than the permitted fatigue capacity as calculated by
the method indicated in Section 3 of CS 456 [Ref 70.N].
NOTE 1
Butt welded reinforcement can be acceptable following a fatigue verification.
NOTE 2
On a heavily trafficked reinforced concrete deck, such as a three lane motorway, the fatigue loading of
a proposed welded joint in reinforcement typically exceeds permitted capacity limits in accordance with
CS 456 [Ref 70.N].
7.67.2
Design of welded joints should be checked against the requirements for permitted welding processes
and examples of application of BS EN 1992-1-1 [Ref 20.N].
7.68
Welded connections of stainless steel reinforcement or dowels shall not be permitted.
63
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

7. Protect and repair strategy
7.69
Where flowable repair concrete is proposed in the design of repairs to soffits, arrangements to prevent
trapped air voids in the repair material shall be included in the design.
NOTE
Arrangement to prevent trapped air voids in the repair material include the following:
1)
inclusion of venting tubes to enable trapped air to be expelled; or
2)
arrangement of formwork shutters to maintain a head of concrete repair material above the
highest point of the repair void to provide the pressure required to expel air voids.
7.70
The execution class for repair work shall be specified in accordance with BS EN 13670 [Ref 21.N].
NOTE
Information and guidance about flowable, hand-applied and sprayed materials and the construction
techniques for applying them can be found on the website: StCA (Website) [Ref 44.I].
7.71
The curing class for repair work shall be specified in accordance with BS EN 13670 [Ref 21.N].
NOTE 1
Proper curing of repaired concrete is very important for development of concrete strength, bond and to
avoid early thermal cracking.
NOTE 2
Advice about curing can be found in CS GCG8 [Ref 23.I].
Non-structural (minor) repairs
7.72
Non-structural repairs shall only be used where the assessed strength of the concrete section is
acceptable when the structural contribution of the repair is ignored.
7.72.1
Non-structural repairs should be limited to small and superficial repairs with areas equal to or less than
0.1 m2 (i.e. 320 mm x 320 mm ) and with an average depth of less than 10 mm .
7.73
Non-structural repairs shall not be located where there is a risk of the repair falling onto the carriageway,
or other surfaces and causing injury to a member of the public, workers or damage to property.
NOTE 1
Non-structural repairs are secured in place only by adhesion of the repair material onto a roughened
substrate.
NOTE 2
There is a risk of injury to the public or damage to property if the repair falls out.
Records
7.74
A record of repairs undertaken on a structure shall be included in the structure file on the management
information system in accordance with CG 302 [Ref 4.N].

8. Internal deterioration of concrete
8.
Internal deterioration of concrete
8.1
Characteristic signs of internal deterioration of concrete (IDC) in a structure shall be investigated.
NOTE 1
IDC in this context means degradation of concrete within the interior of a structural concrete element
which is potentially or actually caused by forms of alkali aggregate reaction (AAR) or delayed ettringite
formation (DEF).
NOTE 2
Typical signs can include map-pattern cracking, surface deposits and there can be leakage through
surface cracks possibly suggesting crack pathways through the concrete core.
8.1.1
Relevant information may be obtained by visual inspection, from archive records of construction
suggesting there are susceptible aggregates combined with high alkali in concrete, or by rapid
expansion testing in accordance with BS 812-123 [Ref 47.I].
8.2
Where IDC is suspected, a special inspection including testing shall be undertaken to confirm its
presence.
NOTE
Information and guidance about AAR can be found in ISE ASR 1992 [Ref 53.N] and ISE ASR 2010 [Ref
52.N].
8.2.1
Delamination and excessive cracking located in areas of high bond or shear stress should be noted.
NOTE
Delamination of cover concrete is rarely caused by IDC and more frequently due to corrosion of
reinforcement.
8.2.2
The diagnosis of IDC should be confirmed as early as possible, to support managing the deteriorated
concrete structure in service.
8.3
The flowchart in Figure 8.3 shall be used as a guide to the steps required for diagnosing IDC and the
procedure to be followed after diagnosis.
65
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
8. Internal deterioration of concrete
Figure 8.3 Investigative steps and the interaction with assessment for a deteriorated
structure suspected of being affected by IDC
8.4
Where IDC is confirmed, the progression of deterioration shall be monitored and recorded.
66
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 68 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
8. Internal deterioration of concrete
8.4.1
Overall structural dimensions and rate of expansion should be measured.
8.4.2
Individual crack widths should also be measured especially at structurally sensitive locations.
8.4.3
The location of existing reinforcement should be surveyed, marked and compared to the pattern of
cracking.
8.5
Where IDC cannot be discounted at this stage of investigation, the affected structure shall be
categorised as "suspected" in accordance with BRE DG 330 [Ref 1.N].
8.6
Concrete from the deteriorated zones shall be sampled and tested to confirm whether IDC is present.
NOTE
Cement content, type of aggregates, sulfate, carbonate and alkali content can be useful information for
confirming the presence of IDC.
8.6.1
Concrete cores should be removed from affected areas and tested to accurately determine current
strength properties.
NOTE 1
Tested values of concrete strength can be variable because of the cracking.
NOTE 2
Testing a small number of samples on a single occasion is not recommended, as results can be
ambiguous.
8.6.2
Samples should be removed from affected areas during at least two consecutive years for improved
accuracy of results.
NOTE
Final confirmation of a reduction in the assessed capacity can therefore be delayed.
8.6.3
Petrographical techniques may be used for examination of concrete cores to confirm the presence of
IDC.
8.6.4
Petrographical examination of core samples should be carried out in accordance with BS 1881-211
[Ref 59.N] and CS TR71 [Ref 10.N].
NOTE
The microscopic examination can provide evidence to help estimation of future deterioration as part of
a long-term structure management plan.
8.7
Sampling and testing for the presence of IDC and reporting shall be undertaken by a specialist testing
laboratory accredited by UKAS for the work required.
8.7.1
IDC should only be declared as the predominant cause of deterioration when other explanations have
been ruled out.
8.8
For structures confirmed as suffering from IDC, an expansion index shall be estimated in accordance
with IStructE guidelines ISE ASR 1992 [Ref 53.N] and ISE ASR 2010 [Ref 52.N].
NOTE
A mild amount of IDC is present where there is an estimated 0.7 mm/m or less of free expansion.
8.8.1
Structures with an expansion index rated "very severe" may require interim measures such as limited
strengthening or load-restrictions or complete replacement.
8.9
Structures confirmed with IDC shall be re-inspected and assessed regularly to monitor deterioration as
part of a long term programme of structure management.
NOTE
There is evidence that progress of IDC does not cease 10 - 15 years after being first identified (see
information in Appendix A of this document). Concrete structures affected by ASR/AAR and built before
the deterioration mechanism was understood and new codes published can continue to be at risk of
deterioration.
8.9.1
The frequency of repeating special inspections and assessment should be in accordance with Table
A.1 in Appendix A.
NOTE 1
IDC damage and cracking are progressive and material properties can deteriorate with time.
NOTE 2
Structures with mild amounts of IDC, i.e. with an estimated 0.7 mm/m or less of free expansion, do not
require assessment.
67
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

8. Internal deterioration of concrete
8.10
The current strength of structural elements confirmed with IDC shall be assessed in accordance with
CS 455 [Ref 69.N].
8.10.1
The sensitivity of a structure to predicted further deterioration should be included as part of the
assessment (see ISE ASR 1992 [Ref 53.N] and ISE ASR 2010 [Ref 52.N]).
8.11
Wherever possible, ground water and other sources of water shall be excluded from contact with parts
of structures suffering from confirmed IDC.
8.11.1
Options for preventing water from getting to vulnerable areas including methods which comply with
Principles 1, 2 or 4 in Table 1 of BS EN 1504-9 [Ref 38.N] may be proposed for consideration by the
TAA.
8.11.2
Options should be chosen to avoid creating hidden defects.
NOTE
Where expansion is severe and large fissures have occurred, the provision of a ventilated cladding can
provide a more sheltered environment.

9. Thaumasite sulfate attack (TSA)
9.
Thaumasite sulfate attack (TSA)
9.1
The risk of TSA occurring on vulnerable structural elements shall be assessed.
NOTE 1
Where a risk assessment report already exists on file, there is no need for further risk assessment
unless circumstances have changed.
NOTE 2
Risk assessment was carried out for structures on most UK strategic roads in the 1990s, and a limited
programme of further investigation was undertaken on structures located in the south west of England.
NOTE 3
Structural elements below ground can be vulnerable where the chemical constituents of foundation
backfill are known to trigger TSA.
NOTE 4
High concentration of sulfates in backfill soil and the availability of water are predominant factors when
reviewing the risk of TSA.
9.1.1
Cores may be taken from parts of substructure elements above ground to determine the aggregate and
cement types present in the concrete.
NOTE 1
Sulfates in groundwater react with carbonates in the concrete aggregate and calcium silicate hydrates
in the cement to form thaumasite. More information can be found in the Thaumasite Expert Group
Report DETR 1999 [Ref 50.I] and BRE SD-1 [Ref 39.I].
NOTE 2
TSA can occur in structures located in areas where the local geology does not include vulnerable
aggregates if transported there and used in concrete mixes.
NOTE 3
Sulfate-resisting Portland cement (SRPC) concretes can also be vulnerable to TSA.
9.1.2
Results and conclusions of the risk assessment should be recorded on file.
9.2
The flowchart in Figure 9.2 shall be used as a guide to the steps necessary to confirm the presence of
TSA, assess the effect of damage and commence the process of damage repair.
69
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 71 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
9. Thaumasite sulfate attack (TSA)
Figure 9.2 Investigative steps for confirming the presence of TSA-affected
structures
9.3
Where a significant risk of TSA is agreed with the TAA, the structure shall be classified as provisionally
70
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 72 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
9. Thaumasite sulfate attack (TSA)
sub-standard and managed in accordance with CS 470 [Ref 31.N].
9.3.1
Management may include monitoring the structure for signs of distress, and at-risk structural elements
below ground can be exposed for inspection, sampling and testing where economical.
NOTE
TSA can cause a reduction in the binding ability of the cement matrix resulting in loss of strength and
structural integrity of the concrete.
9.3.2
The soil surrounding foundations of structures classed at risk may be investigated for evidence of
aggressive chemical compounds and the presence of groundwater.
NOTE 1
Lower Lias clays and made ground containing unburnt or burnt (red) colliery shales are significant
sources of sulfate and can be present as a backfill material around bridge foundations.
NOTE 2
More information can be found in DCLG Thaumasite [Ref 48.I].
9.4
Sampling of concrete and testing shall be undertaken to accurately determine properties of concrete
within the deteriorated zone and confirm whether TSA is present.
NOTE
The most accurate method of determining thaumasite sulfates by mass of cement is through
petrographic examination.
9.5
The severity of concrete deterioration and risk to structural stability shall be assessed where TSA is
confirmed.
9.5.1
The severity of attack should be judged according to the depth of concrete damage (concrete strength
or integrity significantly reduced) and potential for further damage due to high concentration of sulfate in
the surrounding backfill.
NOTE 1
Deterioration to a depth of up to 50 mm after 30 years in service has been uncovered in bridge
sub-structures, constructed from concrete containing Portland cement and carbonate aggregate and
subsequently backfilled using reworked pyritic clay.
NOTE 2
Existing reinforcement can suffer from a combination of general corrosion and deep pitting if TSA and
chloride ion attack are both present.
NOTE 3
The extent and severity of reinforcement corrosion in buried concrete affected by TSA can be low when
there is a limited supply of oxygen, lack of carbonation and chlorides are bound within the cementitious
matrix of concrete.
9.6
Parts of concrete structures confirmed as being affected by TSA shall be repaired in accordance with
the principles and methods in BS EN 1504-9 [Ref 38.N] and following additional requirements:
1)
further investigation to establish the full extent of TSA in the concrete;
2)
removal of affected concrete until the remaining concrete is:
a)
strong enough and free from cracking;
b)
free from visible signs of sulfate products;
c)
has sulfate content of 5% SO42- or less by mass of cement.
NOTE
Current repair protocols for concrete affected by TSA can be found in the Thaumasite Expert Group
Report DETR 1999 [Ref 50.I].
9.6.1
Water should be excluded from parts of the structure suffering from TSA by limiting groundwater
movements, and diverting drains away from affected foundations.
9.6.2
Bituminous coating of repaired and unaffected existing concrete, free-draining backfill and new positive
drainage may also be used to exclude water.
NOTE
Bituminous coatings can have a limited life.
71
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
10.
Design outputs for concrete repairs
General requirements of design outputs for concrete repairs
10.1
The design outputs associated with work-specific requirements for construction of concrete repairs
shall be provided in accordance with this document.
NOTE 1
Work specific requirements for concrete repairs can be found in CC 482 [Ref 51.N].
NOTE 2
Appendix B contains advice on the design outputs for concrete repairs.
10.1.1
The outputs from design of concrete repairs should include details to define the following:
1)
location and extent of concrete areas to be cleaned, inspected, tested, monitored or protected;
2)
location, dimensions, tolerances, setting out, referencing and orientation of concrete elements to
be repaired;
3)
location, setting out, geometry, and tolerances of structural and non-structural repairs
4)
extent of contractor design of repairs;
5)
location, dimensions, setting out, referencing and orientation of concrete elements and
cracks/voids to be repaired;
6)
referencing of concrete repair areas and cracks/voids to be treated;
7)
details showing constraints and other requirements for design of temporary works;
8)
details of designed galvanic anodes within repair areas;
9)
details of any monitoring of galvanic anode systems; and
10)
location and other details of trial panels to demonstrate repair methods, repair methods and other
materials.

Acceptable upper limit of
electrical resistivity of
previously repaired areas to
receive impressed current
cathodic protection within an
overlay
Provide the acceptable upper
limit of electrical resistivity of
previously repaired areas to
receive impressed current
cathodic protection (ICCP)
within an overlay.
Provide value in kΩ.cm .
ICCP systems are typically not
effective where the resistivity of
previously repaired areas is
greater than 150kΩ.cm ,
necessitating the replacement of
the previously repaired areas prior
to the installation of the ICCP
system.
The limit of acceptable electrical
resistivity is based on the advice
given in CPA TN19 [Ref 45.I] of
the Corrosion Prevention
Association (part of the Structural
Concrete Alliance).
Published guidance on testing of
concrete for electrical resistivity
can be found in CS TR60 [Ref
19.N] and Gowers & Millard (ACI)
[Ref 32.I].

10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
Table 10.18 Design outputs for sequencing concrete removal (continued)
Structure ID
Provide the unique identifying
reference of the structure.
The unique reference allocated to each
structure within a scheme, used
consistently throughout the contract.
The reference is from the Overseeing
Organisation's asset management
database.
Structural element
Provide the unique identifying
element reference.
The unique reference allocated to each
structural element within a scheme,
used consistently throughout the
contract.
Sequencing restrictions
Provide the concrete removal
restrictions.
See Section 7 of this document for
requirements and guidance for
maintaining structural safety during
repair work.
Adverse structural effect can be
mitigated by phasing of the repair work,
by propping of the superstructure or by
a temporary restriction of traffic loading.
Concrete removal in a tension zone of
the concrete element can transfer
structural reliance on existing exposed
corroded reinforcement.
Note: the size of repair given in design
outputs can be extended to deal with all
corroded reinforcement, if structural
implications of further breakout are
considered.
For concrete substructure elements
subject to compressive loading, the
removal of defective concrete can result
the transfer of the compressive loading
to the remaining intact concrete with a
resulting concentration of bursting load
effects.
For prestressed concrete elements,
there is a risk of reducing the
pre-stressing forces during removal of
concrete.

Hold points for phased
concrete removal
Provide list of hold points for
phased concrete removal.
See Section 7 of this document for

Hold points are stages of concrete
removal when progress is temporarily
halted to facilitate closer control of
concrete removal process.
Reasons for hold points can include
notification periods, inspection and
recording of the substrate, certification
and agreement for the progression to
the next stage of concrete removal and
any other relevant reasons for pausing
the concrete removal process.

10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
Table 10.20 Design outputs for concrete removal where working around tensioned components
in prestressed and post-tensioned beams
Design output/ reference
Information to be provided as
design outputs
Design requirements, advice
or notes for design outputs
Structure ID
Provide the unique identifying
reference of the structure.
The unique reference allocated
to each structure within a
scheme, used consistently
throughout the contract. The
reference is from the
Overseeing Organisation's
asset management database.
Method(s) of initial concrete
removal permitted for structural
elements containing
prestressing systems
Provide the permitted methods
of initial concrete removal of the
structural elements containing
prestressing systems.
Permitted option limited to:
1) high-pressure water jetting
2) lightweight electric demolition
breaker
3) small hand tools.
See guidance in Appendix B of
this document.
Maximum depth of initial
concrete removal permitted
adjacent to structural elements
containing prestressing
systems
Provide the maximum depth of
initial concrete removal at
surface of concrete elements
containing prestressing
systems.
Provide value in mm .
This is intended to limit the
depth of concrete removal to
mitigate the risk of damage to
the prestressing systems.
Method(s) of concrete removal
permitted in closer proximity to
prestressing systems
Provide the permitted methods
of further concrete removal in
close proximity to prestressing
systems.
Permitted option limited to:
1) high-pressure water jetting
2) lightweight electric demolition
breaker
3) small hand tools.
There is a risk of introducing
micro cracking into pre-stressed
or post-tensioned concrete
members. However there is
also a risk of water penetrating
into unfilled tendon ducts if the
high-pressure water jetting of
concrete continues too close to
the ducts.
Minimum concrete cover to
remain in place when removing
concrete adjacent to
prestressing system
Provide the minimum cover of
concrete to be left around
prestressing systems when
removing concrete in close
proximity.
Provide value in mm .
If concrete is removed beyond
the minimum cover surrounding
live tensioned strands, there is
a risk that the compressive
forces in concrete is reduced
and result in the structural
capacity of the prestressed
element being compromised.

Coefficient of thermal
expansion
Provide whether the concrete
repair product needs to satisfy the
requirement for coefficient of
thermal expansion performance
characteristic in accordance with

Permitted option limited to:
1) Yes
2) No
The coefficient of thermal
expansion characteristic is only
required if repairs are in
contact with a material with a
different thermal expansion
coefficient. e.g. encasement of
structural steel beam.

reinforcement where this is
needed.
Flowable concrete is better than
ordinary structural concrete at
filling irregular shaped repair voids,
and repairs to heavily reinforced
structural elements.
Designed mix sprayed concrete is
not covered by this document.

BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N]
repair product strength class
Provide the strength class of the
repair product in accordance with
BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N].
Permitted option limited to:
1) Class R4
2) Class R3
3) Class R2
The BS EN 1504-3 [Ref
42.N]class of repair product is
chosen so minimum
compressive strength is equal
to or greater than the originally
designed or actual strength of
the existing concrete being
repaired. Only one class of
repair product is specified and
used in each structural element.
Repairs to concrete highway
structures should be designed
as structural. Structural Class
R3 represents a lower concrete
compressive strength than
found on most elements of
modern concrete highway
structures, except for concrete
structures constructed before 1
960. Hence the most commonly
used strength class is expected
to be R4.
Class R2 materials are suitable
only for non-structural repairs
Class R1 is not suitable for
repairs to highway structures.
BS EN 13501-1 [Ref 21.I]fire
class
Provide the performance
characteristics of the repair
product for exposure to fire in
accordance with BS EN 13501-1
[Ref 21.I] and BS 9999 [Ref
22.N].
Permitted option limited to:
1) Class A1
2) Class A2
3) Class B
4) Class C
5) Class D
6) Class E
7) Class F
8) Not applicable
Provide this information where
an assessment of the possible
exposure to fire of concrete
repairs in a location has
identified a high risk of fire
damage, or when fire resistance
is recommended by a design
code of practice.
Refer to BS EN 13501-1 [Ref
21.I]for fire classifications.

at 28 days

/mm2 . Provide value in N

Represented by characteristic

of samples taken from the

Static elastic modulus of

Provide the static elastic modulus
of the existing concrete substrate
to be repaired and identify the
compatibility requirements of the

Provide value in N
/mm2 .
List each element and repair
separately, relating the elastic
modulus to the element and
repair under consideration.
The elastic modulus of a repair
should be similar to the
estimated short-term elastic
modulus of the parent concrete.
Note: elastic behaviour
compatibility can be critical for
structural members
predominantly subjected to
compressive forces in service
e.g. columns and in locations
where there are frequent cycles
of transient loading (see advice
in Section 7 of this document).

Provide value in N
/(mm)2 .
Relevant for phased working to
avoid compromising the
structural integrity of the
structure.

Table 10.31 Design outputs for trial areas to demonstrate methods of executing concrete repairs
Design output/
reference
Information to be provided as
design outputs
Design requirements, advice or
notes for design outputs
Structure ID
Provide the unique identifying
reference of the structure.
The unique reference allocated to
each structure within a scheme,
used consistently throughout the

Overseeing Organisation's asset

Structural element
Provide the unique identifying
element reference.
The unique reference allocated to

In-situ or scale model
Provide whether the trial area is
executed in-situ on the existing
structure or on a scale model.
Permitted option limited to:
1) in-situ
2) scale model
Where repairs are required to many
similar structural elements e.g.
piers, and the pattern of defects to
each pier is similar, full or
part-scale models may be used for
trials to demonstrate the execution
method and performance of the
product under site conditions.
Scale models may be either
existing or newly constructed.
A trial execution is useful to
demonstrate the performance of a
repair product where there is
limited or no evidence or records of
past performance.
Concrete repair
method
Provide the type of trial application.
Permitted option limited to:
1) hand-applied
2) flowable
The repair method used in the trial
execution should reflect the method
proposed for the permanent works.
Design outputs for trial areas for
spray-applied repairs are tabulated
separately.
Number of trial areas
Provide the number of trial areas for
each structural element.
A minimum of one trial area is
required for each proposed method
of concrete repair per structure.
A higher frequency of trial areas
may be provided where the quality
of installation is critical, for
example, repair areas over the live
carriageway.

reinforcement.

straightness profile using the
indicated straightedge
geometrical tolerance on edge
straightness of completed
concrete repairs executed using
flowable or hand applied
materials
Provide the maximum
dimension of repaired edge in
m for checking edge
straightness of repair.
The tolerance for deviation of
edge straightness can be
dependant on the length of the
repair.
Geometrical tolerance on edge
straightness of completed
concrete repair.
Provide the range of
geometrical tolerance
(depressed point to protruding
point) in mm with reference to
the true specified surface edge
straightness profile.
This dimension is the gap or
protrusion between the straight
edge and the concrete edge
being measured.
A negative value for the range
of geometrical tolerance
indicates a depression in the
true straightness profile.
A positive value for the range of
geometrical tolerance indicates
a protrusion from the true
straightness profile.
10.37.1
Site specific geometrical tolerances for the deviation of the edge straightness from the specified profile
of completed repairs executed using flowable or hand applied materials may be provided where there
are scheme specific aesthetic requirements.

development of the surface concrete strength corresponding to the curing class expressed as a % of
the characteristic compressive strength of the concrete.
NOTE 2
A numerically larger curing class requires a longer minimum curing period and develops a higher
surface concrete strength compared to a numerically smaller curing class.

undertaken continuously until

compressive strength class
specified, or until 7 days after
placing the concrete repair,
whichever is later.

Design outputs for electrical resistivity of completed repairs
10.47
The design of concrete repairs including use of type 1B galvanic anodes or an ICCP post-installed over
repairs shall assess whether the maximum electrical resistivity of completed repairs will support
circulation of protective current from the anode system.
NOTE 1
See recommendation and guidance for electrical resistivity of completed repairs in CPA TN19 [Ref 45.I].
NOTE 2
The maximum electrical resistivity of repairs containing type 1B anodes or associated with latter ICCP
system installed later is 100 kΩ.cm according to CC 484 [Ref 11.N].
NOTE 3
The manufacturer of the galvanic anode or ICCP system or the manufacturer's instructions can be
consulted for further guidance.
10.48
Where the maximum permitted electrical resistivity of completed repairs including type 1B galvanic
anodes or a post-installed ICCP system can be different to 100 kΩ.cm , the design outputs shall be
provided in accordance with Table 10.48.

Design output/
reference
Information to be provided as
design output
Design requirements, advice or

Maximum electrical
resistivity of completed
repairs
Provide the maximum electrical
resistivity of completed repairs.
Provide value in kΩcm .
Type 1B anodes are embedded in
existing concrete. The small
anode- generated current flows
through the existing concrete to
protect reinforcement outside the
repair patch. Resistance to current
flow is from the existing concrete.
ICCP can drive a current through new
repair concrete even if the concrete
has relatively high resistivity.
Note: definition of type 1B galvanic
anodes can be found in Section 7 of
this document.
Note: the maximum electrical
resistivity of repairs containing type
1B anodes or associated with latter
ICCP system installed later is given
in CC 484 [Ref 11.N].
Note: guidance on electrical
resistivity of completed repairs can
be found in CPA TN19 [Ref 45.I].
Note: increase of the maximum
electrical resistivity for type 1B
anodes and ICCP can only be done if
the recommended resistivity of
repairs given in CPA TN19 [Ref
45.I]has changed and either of the
following situations apply:
1) type 1B galvanic anodes used or
optional for use within concrete
repairs
2) an ICCP system is being installed
or planned for later.

Proprietary cementitious repair materials should be used for repairing all concrete except where

products in small repair areas.

10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
Table 10.60 Design outputs for the selection of flowable repair concrete or mortar (continued)
Repair material flowability
class
Provide the flowability
performance characteristic of the
repair material.
Permitted option limited to:
1) High flow
2) Normal flow
High flow concrete is suitable
for use within formwork for a
repair void which contains a
dense arrangement of
reinforcement.
Normal flow concrete can be
used where reinforcement is
spaced further apart or
specified for reinstatement of
the upper surface of a steeply
sloping concrete element (e.g.
deck).
Normal flow concrete can be
used where there is a risk that
the "angle of repose" of fresh
high flow concrete is less than
the gradient of the existing
element (e.g. decks with steep
super-elevation > 5% ).
NOTE 1
Methods of applying repair concrete of mortar material can be selected during the construction phase
according to CC 484 [Ref 11.N].
NOTE 2
Refer to Appendix B of this document for advice on design outputs for flowable repair concrete or
mortar.

Sprayed concrete
application process
Provide the required application
process of the repair product.
Permitted option limited to one of :
1) Dry-spray
2) Wet-spray
3) Dry-spray or wet-spray
A repair product formulated for
dry spraying is fed into an air
stream travelling at high velocity
to a nozzle, where water is
added.
A repair product formulated for
wet-spraying is mixed with water
before being discharged from the
nozzle.
Both options for application
process should be selected
unless specific constraints
require use of a particular
application process.
More information can be found
on the SpCA (Website) [Ref
40.I].

classes.

Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 137 of 217

CS 462 Version 0.1.0
10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
Table 10.79 Design outputs for surface finish other than as-sprayed of completed spray-applied
repair concrete or mortar repairs
Design output/ reference
Information to be provided as
design outputs
Design requirements, advice
or notes for design outputs
Surface finish of completed
spray-applied repair concrete
or mortar repairs
Provide the applied surface finish
of completed spray-applied repair
concrete or mortar repairs.
Permitted option limited to:
1) U3 using cut and flash method
2) U3 to first layer using light
trowelling from wooden float
3) U3 to first layer using light
trowelling from steel trowel
Applied finishing, except using
very light trowelling, can cause
plastic cracking.
The U3 cut and flash method
of finish involves trimming the
first layer of sprayed concrete
to true lines, then after an
initial set, spraying again with
an over-wetted flash coat to
produce a textured finish. The
second layer is finished using
a float or trowel.
U3 finishes can also be
achieved for the first layer by
lightly trowelling using a
wooden float or a steel trowel.
10.79.1
The surface finish for spray-applied repair concrete or mortar should be left as-sprayed from the nozzle
unless there is a scheme specific requirement of an alternative surface finish for aesthetic reasons.
NOTE
Finishing of the surface of spray-applied concrete or mortar other than light trowelling can cause plastic
cracking which is detrimental to appearance and durability.

10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
Table 10.85 Design outputs for welding of reinforcement in a bridge deck
Design output/ reference
Information to be provided as
design outputs
Design requirements, advice
or notes for design outputs
Areas where welding of
reinforcement in a bridge deck
is permitted
Provide the areas where
welding of reinforcement in a
bridge deck is permitted.
Check the design of welded
joints against the requirements
for permitted welding processes
and examples of application of
BS EN 1992-1-1 [Ref 20.N].
Butt welding of reinforcement is
the preferred method of welding
bars. If this is unachievable,
fillet welding can be accepted
provided welded joints are only
located in areas where high
cycle fatigue loading is not
expected.
A fatigue verification
assessment is required for fillet
welding of joints where
proposed in fatigue prone
locations (reference BS EN
1992-1-1 [Ref 20.N]) to check
joints can sustain fatigue
loading (see Section 7 of this
document).
Refer to Appendix B of this
document for advice on welding
of reinforcement.
NOTE
Welding of reinforcement can be undertaken in a bridge deck where the applied fatigue loading is less
than the permitted fatigue capacity of the welded joint in accordance with Section 7 of this document.

CS 462 Version 0.1.0
10. Design outputs for concrete repairs
Table 10.97 Design outputs for construction phase design of galvanic anode systems in
concrete repairs (continued)
Design output/
reference
Information to be provided as
design outputs
Design requirements, advice or
notes for design outputs
Monitoring of galvanic
anode system required
Provide whether long-term
performance monitoring of galvanic
anodes within repair is required.
Permitted option limited to:
1) Yes
2) No
Unlike impressed current cathodic
protection, the protective current
supplied by galvanic anodes
cannot be increased, so
monitoring by reference electrode
only confirms a current is being
supplied, and after the galvanic
anode material is consumed,
detect an increase in corrosion
current.
Galvanic anodes should provide a
minimum service life of 10 years,
so the alternative to installing a
positive motioning system is to
inspect the repair areas and
record the re-occurrence of
delamination as indicator of
corrosion starting again.
Where monitoring is
required, type of
monitoring
Where monitoring is required,
provide the type of monitoring.
Permitted option limited to:
1) Not applicable
2) Manual monitoring
3) Remote monitoring
If there is a definite need to
monitor the current being supplied
by the installed anodes,
monitoring points can also be
used to check for indicators that
protection is being achieved.
Maintenance-free
service life of galvanic
anodes
Provide the maintenance-free service
life of galvanic anodes if not 10 years.
Provide value in years.
Consult manufacturers of
galvanic anodes to ascertain if the
proposed longer service life is
feasible for available galvanic
anodes.
Note: the maintenance
free-service life of galvanic anode
systems is at least 10 years
according to CC 484 [Ref 11.N].

Examples of unusual site-specific

permitted from centre of
temporary support to centre of
the existing permanent deck
support.

vulnerable to damage and to

debris arising from local
punch through of deck during
concrete removal.
6) Containment class of
temporary VRS protection to
deck propping at road level.
7) Other temporary measures to
mitigate significant residual
risks identified during design
risk assessment.

Appendix A. Frequency of re-inspection for structures co...
Appendix A. Frequency of re-inspection for structures confirmed with IDC
A1
Introduction
Internal deterioration of concrete (IDC) is caused by various chemical changes resulting in expansion of
the aggregate, which, ultimately results in cracking of the hardened concrete. Causes of IDC include
alkali silica reaction (ASR) now more commonly termed alkali aggregate reaction (AAR) and delayed
ettringite formation (DEF).
BA 52/94 (now withdrawn) provided advice for the assessment and inspection of concrete highway
bridges and other structures which were affected by alkali silica reaction. The advice was based on
contemporary research e.g. Institution of Structural Engineers (IStructE) publication ISE ASR 1992
[Ref 53.N].
Later research, IStructE Addendum 2010 ISE ASR 2010 [Ref 52.N] countermands the original BA
52/94 recommendations and clearly shows how different material properties of concrete are affected to
a different degree by the aggregate expansion, e.g. for 2.5 mm/m of element expansion, typical 28-day
concrete strengths are affected as follows. Compressive strength values show a 20% reduction, tensile
splitting values show a 45% reduction, whilst elastic modulus can be reduced by up to 50%.
Consequently, the theoretical strength equations that use these material parameters are no longer
applicable. These known variations in material properties due to IDC need to be considered in detail as
they will have a direct impact on the behaviour of concrete elements and their assessed capacity.
This change in recommendations i.e. that the internal cracking of the concrete caused by forms of IDC
is progressive and the concrete material properties can deteriorate over time, now requires
re-assessment and reappraisal of structures known to be affected by IDC. This approach is also
confirmed by CS 454 [Ref 5.N].
There is still a need for continuing inspection and assessment of existing concrete structures which are
subject to ASR or other deterioration mechanisms. This supporting document provides guidance on
inspection and testing to be adopted for the monitoring of structures affected by IDC.
A2
Inspection and assessment of concrete suffering from IDC
As it is now known that an initial inspection and assessment of structures with suspected IDC is no
longer adequate, the current condition of the concrete should be determined at appropriate time
intervals.
Inspection and assessment of structures affected by IDC to be undertaken in two phases.
Phase one:
Extraction of cores from affected areas of the structure to obtain actual material properties prior to
assessment of the structural capacity of the elements in question. See CS 455 [Ref 69.N] for advice
about the assessment method.
Cores are extracted using the method in BS EN 12504-1 [Ref 58.N], then tested for:
1)
compressive strength using the method in BS EN 12390-3 [Ref 65.N];
2)
tensile splitting strength using the method in BS EN 12390-6 [Ref 68.N];
3)
secant modulus using the method in BS EN 12390-13 [Ref 67.N].
In addition to the above testing of material properties, petrographic analysis is to be performed on
samples from cores extracted from the structure in accordance with APG APG SR2 [Ref 1.I]. The
petrographic analysis reveals the concrete composition as well as the degradation mechanism and
enables prediction of concrete strength.
Phase two:
Analysis of testing results and assessment - the method is described in CS 455 [Ref 69.N]. The
sensitivity/reactivity of the structure is derived from the petrographic analysis in conjunction with the
worst credible strength results determined from core testing.

Note 1: For grades 1 & 2 the re-test intervals have been stated as 'N/A' because the extent of concrete
degradation is only slight and future significant degradation is not expected unless there is a change in
environmental conditions. Future inspection of the structure can be done at the normal General or
Principal Inspections interval.
Note 2: For grades 3 to 8 continuing core extraction and testing should be carried out at the
recommended time intervals to establish contemporary worst credible strengths of the deteriorated
concrete. This re-testing will demonstrate whether the level of deterioration assumed in the sensitivity
analysis is realistic, but may indicate that the structure is in better condition than predicted.
Note 3: For grades 9 & 10 the re-test intervals have been stated as 'N/A' because the concrete is
evidently in very poor condition and further testing would not provide more useful information.
Measures to repair or replace affected elements are required as a priority.
IDC affects concrete material properties at different rates, the usual equations defining the relationship
between the material properties are not valid. Therefore, when tensile strength or elastic modulus is
required in a structural assessment, it is necessary to apply degradation factors (FDC) which may be
used to derive appropriate values of tensile strength (FDT) and elastic modulus (FDE) for structure
assessment.
By reference to ASTM C856 [Ref 42.I] and ISE ASR 1992 [Ref 53.N] and FDC, the FDT and FDE can be
derived.
A3
Summary
The steps to be taken to determine the concrete properties which reflect the change in condition due to
IDC are set out below:
Obtain sufficient core samples from the structure to allow testing to determine the worst credible
compressive and tensile strengths (WCSdc and WCSdt) together with elastic modulus.
Determine the predicted values for tensile strength and elastic modulus using the codified relationship
between those parameters and the value for worst credible compressive strength. Compare these
predicted values with the test results for these parameters for evidence of IDC.
Carry out phase one of the assessment of the concrete structure in accordance with CS 455 [Ref 69.N],
Technical Guide 9 [Ref 24.N] replacing characteristic concrete material properties within the various
strength equations with those worst credible strength material properties determined by testing.
Carry out petrographic examination of core samples to decide whether any form of IDC has affected
the concrete and to confirm the extent of deterioration, compressive strength degradation factor (FDC)

material properties need to be confirmed again.
Structure assessment activities aligning with these inspection and test activities can be found in CS 454
[Ref 5.N] and CS 455 [Ref 69.N].
