Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
B1
General advice and quality plans for concrete repairs
B1.1
BS EN 1504 terms and definitions for products and systems for the protection and repair of
concrete structures
General terms and definitions useful for the protection and repair of concrete structures are given in BS
EN 1504-1 [Ref 37.N]. More specific terms and definitions can be found in BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N],
BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N], BS EN 1504-6 [Ref 36.N] and BS EN 1504-7 [Ref 40.N].
BS EN 1504-3 [Ref 42.N], BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N], BS EN 1504-6 [Ref 36.N] and BS EN 1504-7
[Ref 40.N] specify requirements and give advice for testing and certification of concrete repair products
and protection and repair of concrete structures. The standards are applicable for repairs to highway
structures constructed of plain concrete, reinforced concrete, pre-stressed concrete, post-tensioned
concrete or a steel/concrete composite. These standards provide manufacturers of repair products with
a framework for obtaining certification for their repair products under the terms of the Construction
Products Regulation.
B1.2
Quality plans for concrete repairs
Concrete repair is a specialist activity requiring a quality sub-plan in accordance with GC 101 [Ref 22.I].
B2
Advice on design outputs for investigations of concrete condition prior to
concrete removal and substrate preparation
B2.1
General advice for construction phase concrete condition investigation
Investigation of the condition of deteriorated concrete structures should be undertaken prior to the
construction phase to inform the design of the repair works.
However, there can be situations where the concrete condition investigation for a whole structure, parts
of a structure or parts of a concrete element cannot be undertaken prior to the construction phase.
Examples can include concrete investigation of a bridge deck top surface normally hidden below road
surfacing and concrete investigation of buried concrete elements when uncovered.
A concrete condition investigation can be undertaken during the construction phase and the results
reported to the Overseeing Organisation to determine the extent of repairs required.
Concrete condition investigation of a whole structure during the construction phase should only be
used as a last resort. This is because the repair design cannot be finalised in advance of construction,
and could result in construction programming complications and an overall cost increase.
Where construction phase concrete condition investigation is required to verify the existing concrete
condition prior to concrete removal, Sections 5 and 10 of this document contain the requirements and
advice for the provision of the design outputs related to concrete condition investigation.
B2.2
Identifying detrimental objects and old repairs prior to installing cathodic protection systems
Where an impressed current cathodic protection (ICCP) system overlying the repaired concrete or a
galvanic system consisting of anodes placed within drilled holes and connected together is proposed,
the investigation of concrete condition should identify metallic objects embedded and visible within the
surface concrete e.g. tying wire, nails, formwork tie bars, unfixed reinforcing bar etc.
These metallic objects can adversely affect the protective current flowing as part of the cathodic
protective system. The metallic objects should be identified, and details of treating these objects shown
on the drawings.
Some unnecessary metal objects can be removed from the concrete and the damage restored.
Treatment of other metal fixings serving a useful purpose can be designed as bonded into the ICCP
system. See guidance in BS EN ISO 12696 [Ref 9.I].

Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
B2.3
Construction phase pre-breakout survey
Where the time interval between the most recent concrete condition survey and the start of repair
works construction is longer than 6 months, there is a risk that the extent of concrete defects has
increased from that shown on the design outputs.
Additional concrete defects identified during a construction phase pre-breakout survey that represent a
greater than 15% increase to the extents of concrete defects has increased from that shown on the
design outputs may require an addendum to the CG 300 [Ref 56.N]Approval in Principle for repairing
the structures.
B3
Advice on design outputs for concrete removal and substrate preparation
B3.1
Historical background of concrete removal
Extensive concrete repair trials were undertaken in 1989 to demonstrate different techniques of
concrete removal. High pressure water jetting (hydro-demolition) was shown as most effective for
removal of concrete and leaving a structurally sound substrate. Concrete substrate resulting from
hydro-demolition can provide a good surface to promote bonding between the concrete substrate and
the repair concrete without the need for additional bonding agents.
B3.1.1
High pressure water jetting
High pressure or ultra-high-pressure water jetting is used in preference to mechanical breakout,
because there is smaller risk of damaging the remaining concrete by introducing micro cracking. Break
out using mechanical tools can cause micro cracking and create a weak layer or potential failure plane
immediately below the broken-out surface. This is also known as concrete bruising.
B3.1.2
Advice for design outputs for concrete removal around tensioned components in prestressed
and post-tensioned beams.
Restrictions and limitations on removing concrete from prestressed or post-tensioned concrete
members are necessary to prevent damage to the prestressing or post-tensioning elements.
Mechanical breakout can cause micro-cracking in the adjacent concrete. Water jetting can cause water
to enter tendon ducts via cracks, tears and joints in the duct material where ducts are not completely
grouted. Water entering and allowed to remain in voided post-tensioned ducts can cause corrosion of
the strands. Ongoing unseen corrosion due to introduction of water into voided ducts during repairs is a
significant risk.
B3.2
Substrate preparation
Preparation of the concrete substrate is undertaken to provide the required bond to the repair material.
Advice about surface profiles achievable by various methods of concrete substrate preparation can be
found in the publication Technical Guideline 310.2R [Ref 38.I] published by the International Concrete
Repair Institute ICRI (website) [Ref 29.I].
Pieces of coarse aggregate in the concrete matrix which are split by a mechanical breaker can reduce
bond of repair materials with the concrete substrate bond because the broken face of some aggregates
is smooth. Records of aggregates used in concrete might not available.
Removal of intact concrete behind the existing reinforcement can release locked-in stresses that
subsequently cause localised delamination.
B3.3
Pre-construction concrete removal trials
Trials of concrete removal prior to construction are beneficial for confirming the effectiveness of the
proposed concrete removal method(s) in the following situations:
1)
concrete to be removed is difficult to access;
2)
concrete surrounds sensitive structural components;
3)
high strength concrete is used in the substrate; or
180
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 182 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
4)
predicted/expected method of concrete removal is unconventional or automated.
B3.4
Hazardous and contaminated materials identified during concrete removal
The presence of hazardous materials can be identified during structure condition surveys, information
gathering, preliminary design or design risk assessment activities, and can be listed in the design
outputs for handling, monitoring and disposal of hazardous materials.
Water arising from the concrete removal by high pressure water jetting will contain particulates, and its
pH will be high. As the water sprayed at high pressure comes into contact with the concrete, the pH of
the water can increase up to a value of 13 which is too high for discharge to a watercourse.
B4
Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
B4.1
Repair products, materials and structural category of repair
The Concrete Society defines non-structural concrete as low strength and only strong enough to cope
with small compressive stresses or temporary loading. For a concrete to be used structurally it is
designed to have a characteristic strength greater than C20/25. Concretes of lesser strength are
termed non-structural.
Repair Guidance Note 3 [Ref 23.N]gives the following advice for structural classification:
1)
Structural concrete is required where repaired element is to be under compressive load, the
elastic modulus, creep and shrinkage should be comparable to the existing concrete it replaces;
2)
Semi-structural concrete can be used where repair product is in contact with reinforcement, but
under no direct compressive loading such as the soffit of a beam. Semi-structural concrete is
unlikely to have same elastic modulus as existing concrete; and
3)
Non-structural concrete can be used where a concrete repair does not extend to reinforcement.
The elastic modulus, creep and shrinkage are unlikely to be the same as existing concrete.
B4.2
Compatibility of construction products with existing concrete
Type CC and Type PCC repair products as defined in BS EN 1504-1 [Ref 37.N]provide chemical and
strain compatibility with the existing concrete, and also restore an alkaline environment around or near
to the existing reinforcement.
Type PC repair products as defined in BS EN 1504-1 [Ref 37.N]do not provide a similar alkaline
environment around or near existing reinforcement as Type CC or Type PCC repair products, hence
use of type PC products is limited to superficial concrete repairs according to CC 484 [Ref 11.N].
B4.3
Repair products used for structures including galvanic anodes or other cathodic protection
systems
Resin-based repair mortars and repair products containing conductive additives, admixtures and fibres
such as steel or carbon impair the performance of the galvanic anode or other cathodic protection
system by:
1)
impeding the current flow due to the high resistivity of polymer resin; or
2)
current deflection caused by conductive additives, admixtures and fibres.
Where ICCP systems are proposed, product used for concrete repairs with electrical resistivity greater
than 150kΩcm can reduce current flow and impair the performance of the ICCP system.
B4.4
Pre-construction concrete repair execution trials
Execution trials of the proposed repair method using can be beneficial to demonstrate the effectiveness
of the proposed concrete repair method where there are doubts about the practicability of achieving full
compaction of concrete placed within formwork or sprayed around congested reinforcement.
181
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 183 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
Trial panels can be used to demonstrate the surface finish and appearance of completed repairs.
B4.5
Quality control of repair work
Quality control tests in accordance with BS EN 1504-10 [Ref 41.N]are used to monitor repair activities
and to confirm that repairs have been implemented effectively. They fall into three categories:
1)
tests on the repair material to demonstrate its workability properties when in the fluid state and
confirm that the batch can achieve its specified compressive strength when hardened;
2)
inspection of the repair area substrate after it has been prepared to confirm execution in
accordance with requirements;
3)
measurements and observations of ambient weather conditions.
Quality control is required initially before the first batch of fresh material is placed into position, and
during placement activities, to indicate consistency of the material and to confirm it has complied with
the strength requirements of the specified class.
B4.6
Unformed surface finish for completed concrete repairs on top of bridge decks
Refer to TRL PPR 376 [Ref 5.I]for advice on the unformed surface finish for completed concrete repairs
on top of bridge decks.
B4.7
Frequency of verification for the installation of concrete repairs
A higher frequency of verification during the early stages of the construction phase for the installation of
concrete repairs can be used to confirm the construction method is reliable and the installed elements
consistently meet the requirements.
B4.8
Protection of a completed repair
Where repairs are carried out on the top surface of a bridge deck, the permanent waterproofing
membrane can protect the new concrete repairs. Waterproofing should not be applied until the moisture
content of the repair has reduced to a the levels required in accordance with CD 358 [Ref 51.I].
B4.9
Curing of a completed repair
Curing of a completed repair that is not undertaken in accordance with BS EN 1504-10 [Ref 41.N]and
the manufacturers instructions increases the risk of early thermal cracking in the immature concrete,
especially when ambient temperatures are high. Refer to Annex F of BS EN 13670 [Ref 21.N] for
advice on methods of curing concrete.
B4.10
Testing electrical resistivity of completed repairs containing galvanic anodes to control
incipient anode effect
BS EN ISO 12696 [Ref 9.I] has a general recommendation that repair products used with
electrochemical reinforcement protection systems should be compatible with and similar to the parent
concrete, however galvanic anodes generate a limited drive voltage, so a limited resistivity of the repair
concrete can be more important for galvanic anode systems.
Advice on testing of concrete for electrical resistivity is provided in CS TR60 [Ref 19.N] and Gowers &
Millard (ACI) [Ref 32.I] Methods of measuring the resistivity of concrete both in the laboratory and in-situ
are provided in RILEM TC-154 [Ref 46.I] where 4-pin, 2-pin and surface electrode to reinforcing cage
methods are discussed along with temperature compensation. Note: RILEM TC-154 [Ref 46.I] states
that a coefficient of variation of 30% in resistivity measurements is expected for tests undertaken in-situ.
B4.11
Cores through completed repairs for verification of repairs
The exact location of cores can be determined on site during the construction phase, using either a
cover meter or by reference to records for the location of the reinforcement.
182
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 184 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
For concrete repairs undertaken using sprayed repair concrete or mortar where samples have not been
taken from trial panels , cores should be taken from the completed repair.
B5
Advice on design outputs for hand applied repair concrete or mortar
B5.1
Use of hand-applied repair concrete or mortar
Hand-applied repair concrete or mortar can be used for reinstatement of small areas or filling core
holes.
B5.2
Use of type PC (reactive polymer) hand-applied repair concrete or mortar
The use of type PC hand-applied repair concrete or mortar is restricted to small and shallow areas to
limit the deformation in the repair patch when subjected to loading. The recommended maximum area
for the use of type PC hand-applied repair concrete or mortar is 0.1m2 . The recommended maximum
depth for the use of hand-applied repair concrete or mortar is 10mm .
On the top surface of bridge decks where type PC hand-applied repair material is overlaid with
bituminous surfacing materials hotter than 100◦C , the hardened resin in the type PC material
temporarily loses its flexural strength as the resin warms to above its glass transition temperature.
B6
Advice on design outputs for flowable repair concrete or mortar
B6.1
Historical background to flowable repair materials
Methods and types of concrete repair mixes were extensively trialled for use on bridge structures in the
West Midlands area in the late 1980s. The 'flowable' concrete term dates to specifications prepared at
that time.
Flowable product formulations contain admixtures which promote a higher consistency at a low
water-cement ratio. Only cementitious BS EN 1504-1 [Ref 37.N] type CC or PCC products can be
described as "flowable". Flowable concrete or mortar works by distributing itself into the void and
compact down to the required density under its own weight without need for further mechanical
compaction apart from light tapping of formwork to remove air trapped at re-entrant corners.
B6.2
High flow and normal flow categorisation of flowable repair materials
Flowable repair concrete or mortar is categorised into high flow and normal flow. High flow repair
concrete or mortar provides a flow length of 750mm or greater within 30 seconds when measured in
accordance with BS EN 13395-2 [Ref 45.N] or BS EN 13395-3 [Ref 46.N]. Normal flow repair concrete
or mortar provides a flow length of 450mm or greater within 30 seconds when measured in accordance
with BS EN 13395-2 [Ref 45.N] or BS EN 13395-3 [Ref 46.N].
B6.3
Repairs to concrete soffits using flowable repair material
Sprayed and hand applied materials are the obvious choices for repairing defects on a concrete soffit.
Sprayed concrete is propelled upwards at speed onto the soffit surface, where the momentum achieves
compaction and the material sticks. Hand applied repair is installed by pressing the material upwards
using hand tools. However, surface finish of repairs executed by these methods can be noticeably
different to the adjacent formed concrete.
Where the surface finish of soffit repairs needs to look the same as existing, flowable concrete can be
used. Compaction of flowable repair materials is assisted by gravity. Compaction can be achieved by
installing the material onto soffit formwork through vertical holes drilled through a deck without
damaging the reinforcement. The detailed method can be designed during the construction phase.
B7
Advice on design outputs for sprayed repair concrete or mortar
B7.1
General advice for sprayed repair concrete or mortar
Either dry-spray or wet-spray application process can be used for sprayed concrete repairs. Where
only one of the two forms of application is proposed during design rather than leaving the choice for the
construction phase, this can be stated in the design outputs.
183
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 185 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
A product formulated for dry spraying is fed into an air stream travelling at high velocity to a nozzle,
where water is added. A product formulated for wet-spraying is mixed with water before being
discharged from the nozzle. More information can be found on SpCA (Website) [Ref 40.I].
Where sprayed repair concrete or mortar is proposed to restore a concrete cross section acting
compositely under variable loading and subject to compressive forces, the elastic (secant) modulus of
hardened repair material should be matched to that of existing concrete (see clause B4.1).
B7.2
Advice on sprayed material test panels
Test panels for demonstrating ability of the sprayed concrete operatives can be constructed in timber
moulds, in-situ on the existing structure, or a small scale reinforced concrete model representing the
existing structure or part of it. The sprayed concrete test panels are undertaken for unreinforced and
reinforced sprayed concrete.
Release agents are not used in timber mould test panels, this is to simulate the restraint to shrinkage
provided by a concrete substrate using the adhesion developed between the concrete and the timber
mould.
B7.3
Sampling, inspection and testing of concrete samples from sprayed concrete test panels
Tests on samples from the sprayed concrete test panels for verifying the competency of the sprayed
concrete operatives, method of installation and properties of the hardened sprayed concrete or mortar
material can include the following:
1)
Inspection for voids, lamination or shadowing behind bars;
2)
Density
3)
Compressive strength;
4)
Elastic (secant) modulus;
5)
Shrinkage; and/or
6)
Electrical resistivity
Testing of electrical resistivity is required where the design includes galvanic anodes within repair
patches or an ICCP system within an overlay is proposed.
B7.4
Inspection category for sprayed repair concrete or mortar
A lower inspection category than Category 3 in accordance with BS EN 14487-1 [Ref 49.N]can be used
where testing for elastic modulus is not required.
B8
Advice on design outputs for steel reinforcement for concrete repairs
B8.1
Treatment of existing reinforcement and structural steel members encased in concrete
For cleaning of reinforcement or structural steel embedded in concrete, surface preparation standard
SA 2 ½ in accordance with BS EN ISO 8501-1 [Ref 35.N] is equivalent to SSPC SP10 / NACE No. 2
[Ref 33.I].
The combination of hydro-demolition of the concrete surrounding the steel reinforcement followed by
wet grit blast cleaning of the steel reinforcement itself can remove surface contaminants and
contaminants within pits and reduce the risk of future steel corrosion. The micro profile imparted to the
steel from this process assists in providing the required bond with the repair concrete.
Preparation of existing steel reinforcement to SA 2 ½ provides the required bond with the repair
material, hence the application of bonding primers is not required unless recommended by the concrete
repair material manufacturer.
The standards of surface finish on cleaned steel reinforcing bars can be enhanced to a an higher
abrasive blasting standard SA3 white steel.
184
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 186 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
Hand cleaning of existing reinforcement can also be used when the available time constraints imposed
on the work are very onerous, but should be limited to small areas as the prepared surface finish of
hand preparation standards is not as uniform as abrasive blasting.
For repairs to the concrete-encasement of structural steel members, surface preparation of steel can
be by abrasive blasting or hand preparation.
A bonding primer can be used to achieve an improved bond between the encased steel members and
the repair material. A bonding primer should be shown on the drawings. The bonding primer can be an
integral part of the proprietary repair system.
B8.2
Additional or replacement reinforcing bars
High chloride contamination in existing concrete structures can cause pitting corrosion and section loss
to existing reinforcement within the structure.
Additional reinforcement bars can be provided within the concrete repair to maintain the structural
capacity of a concrete element where the existing reinforcement is found to have pitting corrosion and
section loss.
Stainless steel reinforcement is not used in concrete repairs due to the risk of bimetallic corrosion with
the existing carbon steel reinforcement, particularly in the splash zones where corrosion has occurred
previously.
B8.3
Reinforcement couplers
Where reinforcement couplers are proposed in the design, fatigue capacity checks for the proposed
couplers should be undertaken in relation to the applied stress range and number of expected cycles of
that stress range during the remaining life of the structure.
Where steel couplers are proposed in the design for splicing existing reinforcement with imperial size
diameter and new metric size reinforcement, the coupler manufacturer should be consulted about
whether the mix of reinforcement sizes affects the certified strength properties of the chosen coupler.
B8.4
Splicing replacement or additional reinforcing bars
Space restrictions within areas of concrete repair can prevent the splicing or coupling of reinforcement
bars, leaving welding of replacement bars as the only viable option for the installation of replacement or
additional reinforcement.
B9
Advice on design outputs for galvanic anodes used for control of incipient
anode effect
B9.1
General advice on galvanic anodes for control of incipient anode effect
Galvanic anodes work by generating a protective current when attached to existing reinforcement due
to the steel and the metal used in the galvanic anode having different electrochemical potentials. The
metallic core of the galvanic anode sacrificially corrodes to protect the steel reinforcement to which it is
connected.
Galvanic anodes and reference electrodes rely for their operation on the free flow of electrons within
the electrochemical circuit consisting of the anodes, concrete and reinforcement. The circuit relies on
electrical continuity between the anode or reference electrode and the existing steel reinforcement.
Galvanic anodes used within repair patches can reduce the extent of intact but contaminated concrete
adjacent to the area of delaminated concrete that needs to be broken out. See requirements and
advice in Section 7 of this document.
B9.2
Construction phase design of galvanic anodes for control of incipient anode effect
Information to be provided as design outputs for the construction phase design of galvanic anode
systems can be obtained from existing reinforcement drawings and concrete testing reports.
185
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025



======================================================================
PAGE 187 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 Version 0.1.0
Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
Information to be provided should include:
1)
Steel reinforcement density ratio;
2)
Chloride ion concentration by weight of cement;
3)
If monitoring of galvanic anode system is required;
4)
Maintenance free service life of anodes;
B9.3
Types of galvanic anodes for control of the incipient anode effect
Definition of type 1A and type 1B galvanic anode types can be found in CC 484 [Ref 11.N].
B9.4
Type 1A galvanic anodes
Refer to CPA (website) [Ref 17.I] for advice on the electrical resistivity of repair mortar or concrete used
in concrete repairs where type 1A galvanic anodes are proposed.
B9.5
Monitoring of galvanic anode systems for control of incipient anode effect
Unlike impressed current cathodic protection, the protective current supplied by galvanic anodes
cannot be increased, so monitoring by reference electrode only confirms a current is being supplied,
and after the galvanic anode material is consumed, detects an increase in corrosion current.
Galvanic anodes used within repairs to control the incipient anode effect are not commonly monitored.
Testing following installation confirms the galvanic anodes are working.
Galvanic anodes are required to have a serviceable life of 10 years or more, so when galvanic anodes
are theoretically life expired, delamination in concrete adjacent to the anode protected repair patch
adjacent concrete can be a sign the anodes have stopped working.
B9.6
Reference electrodes and other components for monitoring galvanic anodes used within
repairs.
If there is a definite need to monitor the current being supplied by the installed galvanic anodes, a
monitoring system can be installed.
Information to be provided as design outputs for the construction phase design of galvanic anode
monitoring systems can include:
1)
Type of monitoring where required;
2)
Type and service life of reference electrodes where required;
3)
Type and service life of junction boxes;
4)
Details of single core cables.
Reference electrodes in galvanic anode systems are used to confirm that an electrical current is being
generated by the galvanic anode system such that corrosion protection of the reinforced concrete is
provided, and detect an increase in corrosion current after the galvanic anode material is consumed.
It can take 3 - 4 months for the current developed by galvanic anodes to distribute itself throughout the
reinforcement cage, achieve polarisation and provide full protection to reinforcement outside the repair
patch.
Reference electrodes can function correctly for at least 20 years. Readings from the reference
electrode monitoring system should remain stable regardless of variations in concrete temperature.
Refer to BS EN ISO 12696 [Ref 9.I]for advice.
Where reference electrodes are required for monitoring corrosion current, they should be located within
the existing intact concrete, as advised by BS EN ISO 12696 [Ref 9.I]. The location of reference
electrodes should be shown on the drawings.

Appendix B. Advice on design outputs for concrete repairs
B10
Advice on design outputs for filling or injecting cracks & voids in existing
concrete
B10.1
Concrete injection
Refer to CS TR69 [Ref 48.N]for advice on filling or injecting cracks and voids in concrete.
Cracking in the existing concrete can occur where the steel reinforcement does not provide the
structural capacity to resist cracking in immature concrete. This can be evident in structures
constructed prior to the publication of crack control design codes and standards.
B10.2
Products for concrete injection or filling
BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N] defines the function of crack injection products as:
1)
capable of transmitting force (F);
2)
remaining ductile (D);
3)
swelling to fill the crack (S).
BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N]defines the binder types used in concrete injection products as:
1)
hydraulic cementitious binder (H); and
2)
reactive polymer binder (P).
Note that only a cementitious injection product (binder type H) restores full passivity to reinforcement
bars exposed in a crack.
Refer to BS EN 1504-5:2004 [Ref 39.N] for additional performance characteristics "for certain intended
uses" which include the following:.
1)
adhesion by slant shear strength;
2)
glass transition temperature;
3)
chloride content;
4)
expansion ratio;
5)
water tightness;
6)
corrosion behaviour.
B10.3
Preparation of cracks
BS EN 1504-10 [Ref 41.N], Annex A.4 contains advice about the preparation of cracks and joints prior
to crack filling using injection, soaking or vacuum.
B10.4
Execution of concrete injection
BS EN 1504-10 [Ref 41.N], Annex A.4 contains advice about techniques of concrete injection. Further
information and advice can be found in CS TR69 [Ref 48.N].
B11
Advice on design outputs for temporary works for concrete repairs
CC 484 [Ref 11.N]and CG 300 [Ref 56.N] describes the technical approval documentation required
temporary works for concrete repairs. Temporary works for concrete repairs include temporary support
of structural members or temporary access to repair areas.

Release notes
2
Foreword
3
Publishing information . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
3
Contractual and legal considerations . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . .
3
Introduction
4

CS 462 - WNAA Version 1.0.0
Introduction
Introduction
Background
This National Application Annex document contains requirements on the preparation of Structure
Management Options Reports (SMORs) that are additional to the requirements stated in CS 462 [Ref
6.N]. The purpose of these additional requirements is to achieve consistency in SMORs submitted by
suppliers and any deviations should be discussed and agreed with the TAA prior to submission.
This guidance note contains requirements on the structure of SMORs, report content and corrosion risk
assessment, whole-life cost assessment, option selection and technical approval requirements.
Assumptions made in the preparation of this document
The assumptions made in GG 101 [Ref 4.N]apply to this document.
4
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

W/1. SMOR Guidance
W/1.
SMOR Guidance
Report structure
W/1.1
The main structure of SMOR documents shall follow the table of contents proposed below that is
aligned with CS 462 [Ref 6.N].
Table W/1.1 SMOR Table of Contents

4

6

6.2
Structural element B
6.3
....

Detailed whole-life cost analysis
Report Content and Corrosion Risk Assessment
W/1.2
The scope of works, extent of concrete and other repairs to be assessed, shall be clearly stated in the
introduction.
W/1.3
Structural elements that are out of scope but are critical and at risk of deterioration shall be highlighted
and recommendations for further investigations included.
W/1.4
The concrete testing strategy shall be representative of the whole structure and include deteriorated
areas where the chloride content is critical to the design of the repair method.
W/1.4.1
Test panels in deteriorated areas where the chloride content is critical to the design of the repair
method should be in addition to the location of test panels recommended in CS 462 [Ref 6.N].
W/1.5
The inspection, survey and testing results shall be clearly summarised and presented within the report.
W/1.5.1
The latest concrete testing report(s) should be included in the appendices that cover all results
summarised in the main body of the SMORs.
W/1.6
The report shall contain a summary of both advantages and disadvantages for each option and highlight
risks potentially affecting functionality and effectiveness of each option throughout the whole-life cycle.
Whole-Life Cost Assessment
W/1.7
The whole-life cost assessment shall be impartial.
W/1.8
The life-cycle input parameters, including budget costs and expected design lives, shall be clearly
stated within the report and summarised in a table.
W/1.9
The source of the input data shall be referenced.
W/1.9.1
Where there is a range of budget costs per area in published literature then a consistent approach
should be adopted or mean as well as lower and upper boundary values provided.
NOTE
An example of where a range of budget costs per area is published in literature is CPA TN12 [Ref 1.I].
W/1.10
The whole-life cost analysis results shall be clearly presented using the terms and definitions from CD
355 [Ref 1.N].
8
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

PAGE 211 of 217
======================================================================
CS 462 - WNAA Version 1.0.0
W/1. SMOR Guidance
W/1.11
The 'user delay', 'initial', 'life-cycle' and 'present value' costs shall be clearly summarised in table format.
Options Selection
W/1.12
In addition to the whole-life cost assessment results, the final option selection shall be based on
technical, environmental, sustainability and health & safety justifications.
W/1.12.1
The technical, environmental, sustainability and health & safety justifications should be included in the
discussion sections following the whole-life cost analysis.
W/1.13
The option scoring system proposed in this Section shall be adopted.
Sustainability
W/1.14
A high-level quantitative and qualitative sustainability assessment shall be carried out for all options.
W/1.14.1
The embodied carbon of the main aspects of the option, such as concrete removal, reinstatement,
anode materials / mass and equipment to be used, should be quantitatively estimated to inform the
overall option scoring.
NOTE
The organisation carrying out the estimation can use their own or third party tools to quantitatively
estimate embodied carbon.
W/1.14.2
The impact of traffic delays and restrictions on the public should be qualitatively assessed by taking into
account the traffic user-delay cost calculations in CD 355 [Ref 1.N].
W/1.15
A carbon assessment shall be undertaken for the top three or 40% of scored options, whichever is
higher.
W/1.15.1
The result of the assessment should be included in the discussion and justification of the option
selection.
Technical Approval Requirements / Competencies
W/1.16
Where SMOR options are progressed to design, the following shall be classified as Category 3
proposals and subject to checking procedures in accordance with CG 300 [Ref 7.N]:
1)
for options other than conventional concrete patch repairs.
2)
for galvanic and impressed current cathodic protection systems including hybrid cathodic
protection systems, and other electrochemical techniques to prevent or protect against corrosion
that are not explicitly included in CC 484 [Ref 3.N].
3)
where the structure is affected by alkali-silica reaction.
4)
where the cathodic protection system is installed in the vicinity of half-joints.
W/1.17
Where SMOR options are progressed to design and implementation, the personnel undertaking or
approving the design of cathodic protection systems and monitoring of cathodic protection systems
shall be certificated to BS EN ISO 15257 [Ref 2.N] Level 4 in reinforced concrete.
Scoring System to Assess SMOR Options
W/1.18
The scoring system given in Table W/1.18 shall be used to assess SMOR options.
9

PAGE 214 of 217

CS 462 - WNAA Version 1.0.0
W/1. SMOR Guidance
NOTE 1
Qualitative / non-measurable assessment: Individual criteria score derived for items which are not
directly measurable from a generic scoring scale: 0 to 10 (0 = Unacceptable … 10 = Excellent / Most
suitable)
NOTE 2
Quantitative / measurable assessment: Individual criteria score derived from scaling items that are
directly quantifiable using Equation W/1.18N2.
Equation W/1.18N2 Quantitative / measurable assessment rating
S =
x −x min
x max −x min · 10
where:
x =
Value of quantitative factor for SMOR option - i.e. cost / TM time / time onsite / carbon
xmax =
Maximum value of quantitative factor across SMOR options - i.e. cost / TM time / time
onsite / carbon
xmin =
Minimum value of quantitative factor across SMOR options - i.e. cost / TM time / time
onsite / carbon
12
Downloaded from https://www.standardsforhighways.co.uk on 12-Nov-2025, CS 462 Version 0.1.0, published: 30-Sep-2025

W/2.
Normative references
The following documents, in whole or in part, are normative references for this document and are
indispensable for its application. For dated references, only the edition cited applies. For undated
references, the latest edition of the referenced document (including any amendments) applies.
Ref.
Document
Ref 1.N
National Highways. CD 355, 'Application of whole-life costs for design and
maintenance of highway structures'

protection persons – Basis for certification scheme'

Bridges'
Ref 5.N

structures. Definitions, requirements, quality control and evaluation of conformity.
General principles for use of products and systems'

highway structures'
